<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome sequence of first monocot-halophytic species Oryza coarctata reveals stress-specific genes"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2018 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-09-12"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Oryza coarctata (KKLL; 2n&#8201;=&#8201;4x&#8201;=&#8201;48, 665&#8201;Mb) also&#160;known as Porteresia coarctata is an extreme halophyte species of genus Oryza. Using Illumina and Nanopore reads, we achieved the assembled genome size of 569.9&#8201;Mb, accounting 85.69% of the estimated genome size with N50 of 1.85&#8201;Mb and 19.89% repetitive region. We also found 230,968 simple sequence repeats (SSRs) and 5,512 non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs). The functional annotation of predicted 33,627 protein-coding genes and 4,916 transcription factors revealed that high salinity adaptation of this species is due to the exclusive or excessive presence of stress-specific genes as compared to rice. We have identified 8 homologs to salt-tolerant SOS1 genes, one of the three main components of salt overly sensitive (SOS) signal pathway. On the other hand, the phylogenetic analysis of the assembled chloroplast (134.75&#8201;kb) and mitochondrial genome (491.06&#8201;kb) favours the conservative nature of these organelle genomes within Oryza taxon."/>

        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Subjects</h3>
        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">
            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-evolution" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant evolution</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-sciences" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant sciences</a></li>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Oryza coarctata</i> (KKLL; 2n = 4x = 48, 665 Mb) also known as <i>Porteresia coarctata</i> is an extreme halophyte species of genus <i>Oryza</i>. Using Illumina and Nanopore reads, we achieved the assembled genome size of 569.9 Mb, accounting 85.69% of the estimated genome size with N50 of 1.85 Mb and 19.89% repetitive region. We also found 230,968 simple sequence repeats (SSRs) and 5,512 non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs). The functional annotation of predicted 33,627 protein-coding genes and 4,916 transcription factors revealed that high salinity adaptation of this species is due to the exclusive or excessive presence of stress-specific genes as compared to rice. We have identified 8 homologs to salt-tolerant <i>SOS1</i> genes, one of the three main components of salt overly sensitive (<i>SOS</i>) signal pathway. On the other hand, the phylogenetic analysis of the assembled chloroplast (134.75 kb) and mitochondrial genome (491.06 kb) favours the conservative nature of these organelle genomes within <i>Oryza</i> taxon.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Soil salinity is a major abiotic stress reducing the yield of rice cultivated globally and is increasing gradually due to surface irrigation, high cropping intensity and cultivation of high yielding variety<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Molla, K. A., Debnath, A. B., Ganie, S. A. &amp; Mondal, T. K. Identification and analysis of novel salt responsive candidate gene based SSRs (cgSSRs) from rice (Oryza sativa L.). BMC Plant Biol. 15, 122, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/s12870-015-0498-1&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12859e424">1</a></sup>. In general, rice is a glycophyte species except few tolerant landraces which can grow up to a maximum limit of 12 E.Ce (electrical conductivity) dS/m<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ganie, S. A. et al. Assessment of genetic diversity of Saltol QTL among the rice (Oryza sativa L.) genotypes. Physiol. Mol. Biol. Plants 22, 107–114, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s12298-016-0342-6&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12859e428">2</a></sup>. Those landraces have been extensively used by the plant breeders to identify salt tolerant QTLs for introgression to popular salt sensitive genotypes of rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Thomson, M. J. et al. Characterizing the saltol quantitative trait locus for salinity tolerance in rice. Rice 3, 148–160, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s12284-010-9053-8&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12859e432">3</a></sup>. Thus genetic potential for salt tolerance among the existing population has been fully exploited. Therefore, alternative effectual alleles from wild species may aid in amending the salinity tolerance to an advanced level. Wild species are a potential alternative source of many useful alleles that may not be present in the primary gene pool of the domesticated species.</p><p><i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>, an allo-tetraploid species (chromosome number of 2n = 4X = 48, KKLL genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lu, F. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of MONOCULM1-orthologous regions in 14 Oryza genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2071–2076, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.0812798106&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12859e444">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Sanchez, P. L., Wing, R. A. &amp; Brar, D. S. The wild relative of Rice: Genomes and genomics. In: Genetics and Genomics of Rice 9–25, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4614-7903-1_2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12859e447">5</a></sup> is popularly known as Asian wild rice, grows naturally in the coastal region of South-East Asian countries, where this plant experiences the lunar tide and is exposed to submerge saline sea water in every alternative 12 h. It set flowers and seeds under high saline conditions of upto 40 E.CedS m<sup>−1</sup> saline soil<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bal, A. R. &amp; Dutt, S. K. Mechanism of salt tolerance in wild rice (Oryza coarctata Roxb). Plant and Soil 92, 399–404, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02372487&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1986)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d12859e453">6</a></sup>. It is the only halophytic species in the genus <i>Oryza</i> and represents the evolutionary transition of aquatic plant to land plant because of the presence of ancient as well as advanced characteristics of terrestrial habitat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Frew, P. E. &amp; Bowen, R. H. Memoirs: Nucleolar behaviour in the mitosis of plant cells. Quarterly J. Microscopical Sci. 2, 197–212, &#xA;                    http://jcs.biologists.org/content/s2-73/290/197&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1929)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12859e461">7</a></sup>. Although several pinitol biosynthesis pathway genes individually have been cloned for functional genomics study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sengupta, S. &amp; Majumder, A. L. Insight into the salt tolerance factors of a wild halophytic rice, Porteresia coarctata: A physiological and proteomic approach. Planta 229, 911–929, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s00425-008-0878-y&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12859e465">8</a></sup> but there is lack of large scale genomics resources for this species except one study each on transcriptome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Garg, R. et al. Deep transcriptome sequencing of wild halophyte rice, Porteresia coarctata, provides novel insights into the salinity and submergence tolerance factors. DNA Res. 21, 69–84, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/dnares/dst042&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12859e469">9</a></sup> and on microRNA (miRNAs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mondal, T. K., Ganie, S. A. &amp; Debnath, A. B. Identification of novel and conserved miRNAs from extreme halophyte, Oryza coarctata, a wild relative of rice. PLoS ONE 10, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0140675&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12859e473">10</a></sup>. Apart from these, some BAC end sequences and repeat libraries for <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> were also reported as part of The Oryza Map Alignment Project (OMAP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus. Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.3766306&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2006)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12859e484">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zuccolo, A. et al. Transposable element distribution, abundance and role in genome size variation in the genus Oryza. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2148-7-152&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d12859e484_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. D. et al. The Oryza BAC resource: A genus-wide and genome scale tool for exploring rice genome evolution and leveraging useful genetic diversity from wild relatives. Breeding Sci. 60, 536–543, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1270/jsbbs.60.536&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12859e487">13</a></sup> but no efforts seems to be reported to decode the genome assembly of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>, which is the pre-requisite to harness its genetic potential for salinity tolerance. There are 24 morphologically distinct species in the genus <i>Oryza</i>, yet 11 of them have been sequenced till today though none of them are halophyte in nature. Genome sequence has several advantages such as i) generation of large number of markers which in turn help in marker assistant breeding, ii) revealed several genomic resources both for coding and non-coding region and hence can be used for evolutionary study by comparative genomics, iii) helps to identify the conserved and novel genes along with paralogs, hence providing better opportunity to select the gene of interest.</p><p>Most of the work done so far on the salinity genes have been employed on model but glycophytic species i.e. rice and <i>Arabidopsis</i> which have very less or no significance to endure high salinity. Recently, few halophytes have been sequenced such as <i>Thellungiella parvula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913–918, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1038/ng.889&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d12859e508">14</a></sup>, <i>T</i>. <i>salsuginea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wu, H. J. et al. Insights into salt tolerance from the genome of Thellungiella salsuginea. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 109, 12219–24, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1209954109&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12859e517">15</a></sup>, <i>Eutrema salsugineum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Yang, R. et al. The reference genome of the halophytic plant Eutremasalsugineum. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 46, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2013.00046&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12859e524">16</a></sup>, but none of them are monocot in nature. Being a close wild relative of one of the most important cultivated crop world-wide i.e. rice, and monocot in nature, <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> may aid to generate an important and large reservoir of salinity tolerant genes which, either through conventional breeding or through genetic engineering approaches, will help research community for improving salinity tolerance in rice plants.</p><p>In the present study, we have decoded the whole genome of a halophytic monocot species <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> using Illumina and Nanopore reads to generate a high-quality (HQ) draft genome assembly of 569.9 Mb with a scaffold N50 of 1.85 Mb. We also assembled and reported the complete sequence of chloroplast and mitochondrial genome of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>. We discovered many stress-specific or salinity responsive genes, including <i>SOS1</i> (salt overly sensitive 1) genes, in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> which are either missing or present in low copy number in the rice. Evolutionary and syntenic studies have been performed at genome level, organelle level and gene level to unravel not only the conserved features of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> within <i>Oryza</i> genus but also to uncover its similarity with other halophytic species and differences from AA, BB and FF genome types of the genus <i>Oryza</i>. The genomic resources developed in this sequencing effort should contribute in advancing molecular breeding programmes against salinity and to investigate the <i>Oryza</i> genome evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Materials and Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Materials and Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome size estimation</h3><p>The plant, after collecting from the coastal region of Sundarban delta of West Bengal, India (21°36′N and 88° 15′E) was established at Net house through clonal propagation. To estimate the genome size, we used 20 mg leaf extract from a 10 cm long plant grown in pot. DNA content was estimated as fluorescence of propidium iodide (PI)-stained<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Dolezel, J., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Suda, J. Estimation of nuclear DNA content in plants using flow cytometry. Nat. Protoc. 2, 2233–44, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1038/nprot.2007.310&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12859e587">17</a></sup> nuclei of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome using <i>Pisum sativum</i> (pea) (1 C = 9.09 pg) as an external standard<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Baranyi, M. &amp; Greilhuber, J. Flow cytometric analysis of genome size variation in cultivated and wild Pisum sativum (Fabaceae). Plant Syst. Evol. 194, 231–239, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/BF00982857&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1995)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12859e600">18</a></sup>. Experiment was conducted with FACS cell sorter by BD-LSR II(BD-JH FACS Academy, Jamia Hamdard (Hamdard University) Hamdard Nagar, New Delhi, India)and data were analyzed by BD FACS Diva v.8.0.1 (<a href="http://www.bdbiosciences.com/in/instruments/software/facsdiva/features/overview.jsp">http://www.bdbiosciences.com/in/instruments/software/facsdiva/features/overview.jsp</a>). The whole experiment was repeated 3 times with 8 samples in each time.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec4">Library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA (gDNA) was isolated by CTAB method as per our previous protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ganie, S. A. et al. Assessment of genetic diversity of Saltol QTL among the rice (Oryza sativa L.) genotypes. Physiol. Mol. Biol. Plants 22, 107–114, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s12298-016-0342-6&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12859e620">2</a></sup> from young leaf of the same plant used to estimate the genome size. The quality of the isolated DNA was checked by a NanoDrop D-1000 spectrophotometer (NanoDrop Technologies, Wilmington, DE) and Qubit Fluorometer. This DNA was used to make one Illumina short Paired-end (PE) library of 151 bp long reads and four Mate-pair (MP)library such as 2 kb, 4 kb, 6 kb and 8 kb in size following the standard Illumina protocols (Illumina, San Diego, CA) and sequenced with HiSeq4000 platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA). In addition, we also used Nanopore long reads for better assembly which was sequenced on MinION Mk1b (Oxford Nanopore Technologies, Oxford, UK) using SpotON flow cell (R9.4) in a 48 h sequencing protocol on MinKNOW 1.4.32. Base calling was performed using Albacore and base called reads were processed using Poretools version 0.6.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Loman, N. J. &amp; Quinlan, A. R. Poretools: A toolkit for analyzing nanopore sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 3399–3401, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu555&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12859e624">19</a></sup>. All the sequencing works were carried out at M/S Genotypic Technology Private Limited, Bengaluru, India.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec5">
                           <i>De-novo</i> assembly of nuclear genome</h3><p>First, the raw reads were quality checked using FastQC_v.0.11.5 (<a href="https://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/">https://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/</a>) and were further processed by PLATANUStrimmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384–1395, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.170720.113&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12859e647">20</a></sup> for trimming the adaptors and low quality regions towards 3′-end at PHRED quality score cut-off of Q30. The processed paired-end (PE) reads were used for contig assembly using PLATANUS <i>de novo</i> assembler v1.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384–1395, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.170720.113&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12859e654">20</a></sup> at default parameters. Scaffolding of the assembled contigs was performed using PLATANUS v1.2.4 and SSPACE (<a href="https://www.baseclear.com/genomics/bioinformatics/basetools/SSPACE">https://www.baseclear.com/genomics/bioinformatics/basetools/SSPACE</a>, V3.0) with MP and Nanopore libraries followed by gap closing with GapCloser program (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/about.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/about.html</a>) to fill the gaps within scaffolds.</p><p>Completeness of the assembled genome was evaluated by Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA) (<a href="http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/datasets/cegma/">http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/datasets/cegma/</a>) and Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) (<a href="http://busco.ezlab.org/">http://busco.ezlab.org/</a>) with <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> as the reference genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec6">Genome annotation of nuclear genome</h3><p>Putative protein-coding genes were predicted using a combined strategy that integrates <i>ab intio</i> gene predictor AUGUSTUS 3.1 (<a href="http://bioinf.uni-greifswald.de/augustus/">http://bioinf.uni-greifswald.de/augustus/</a>) and sequence evidence based annotation pipeline, MAKER-P v2.31.8 (<a href="http://www.yandell-lab.org/software/maker-p.html">http://www.yandell-lab.org/software/maker-p.html</a>) with <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> as reference gene model. In addition, 5 transcriptome SRA [SRX248542 (Salt450 along with Submerged); SRX248538 (Control); SRX248541 (Submerged); SRX248540 (Salt700); SRX248539 (Salt450)] were used as expression evidence for this prediction (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1</a>). Resulting gene models were filtered for valid start codon. The final set of predicted protein-coding genes was annotated with Blast2GO (version 4.01)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Conesa, A. &amp; Götz, S. Blast2GO: A comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics 2008, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1155/2008/619832&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12859e737">21</a></sup> by using BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1016/S0022-2836(05)80360-2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1990)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d12859e741">22</a></sup> based approach against a database containing functional plant genes downloaded from NCBI with an E-value cut-off of ≤1e<sup>−10</sup>. Genes with significant hits were assigned with GO (Gene Ontology) terms and EC (Enzyme Commission) numbers. InterProScan search and pathway analyses with KEGG database were also performed by using Blast2GO.</p><p>In order to predict the conserved and unique gene families among the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome and 11 other <i>Oryza</i> species, an HMMScan of HMMER 3.1 package (<a href="http://hmmer.org/">http://hmmer.org/</a>) was run against the protein sequences of all these 12 species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1</a>), based on the profiles compiled in Pfam release 31.0 (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/">http://pfam.xfam.org/</a>) of the Pfam database at default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec7">Prediction and analysis of F-box and SOS pathway genes</h3><p>A combined approach of HMMER search and BLAST search was used to retrieve the putative F-box genes. F-box HMM domain (PF00646) was downloaded from Pfam site (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/">http://pfam.xfam.org/</a>). HMMER search and BLAST search was done against 33,627 protein sequences of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> with the E-value ≤ 1e<sup>−10</sup>. Both results were merged for non-redundant significant hits and were checked for the presence of F-box domain (PF00646) by CD search tool (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Structure/bwrpsb/bwrpsb.cgi">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Structure/bwrpsb/bwrpsb.cgi</a>).</p><p>A keyword based search was done for available <i>SOS1</i> genes at NCBI with keyword “plasma membrane Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter” or “<i>SOS1</i>” or “salt overly sensitive 1”. A non-redundant set of 149 <i>SOS1</i> genes was found in different plant species, ranging from a minimum length of 65 amino-acid to maximum 1213 amino-acid. Out of total 149 genes, 123 genes were found to have the “Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter or exchanger family” domain as predicted by CD-batch search tool. To predict the <i>SOS1</i> genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome, the 33627 genes of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> were BLAST searched against these 123 <i>SOS1</i> genes. The significant hits, with 60% identity, were checked for the presence of “Sodium/hydrogen exchanger family” domain (PF00999) at threshold of e-value ≤ 1e-<sup>10</sup> by CD search tool. MEGA 7.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis Version 7.0 for Bigger Datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1870–1874, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msw054&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12859e850">23</a></sup> was used to perform alignment and phylogenetic analyses between the newly predicted <i>SOS1</i> genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> and known <i>SOS1</i> genes from other plant species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec8">Identification of transcription factors</h3><p>To identify the transcription factors (TFs) in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genes, plant transcription factors sequences were downloaded from Plant Transcription Factor Database v4.0 (<a href="http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/">http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/</a>) and a BLAST search based approach was used with cut-off values for E-value, identity and query-coverage as ≤1e<sup>−10</sup>, ≥40% and ≥50%, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hittalmani, S. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequence of Finger millet (Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.) provides insights into drought tolerance and nutraceutical properties. BMC Genomics 18, 465, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d12859e890">24</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">Identification of repetitive elements and SSR markers</h3><p>Repetitive elements, retrotransposons and DNA transposons were identified and masked in the assembled <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome by using RepeatMasker Tool (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) against the RepBase v.20170127 (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase/">http://www.girinst.org/repbase/</a>) using the reference genomic repeats of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> for hard masking. LTR_FINDER v1.05 was used to identify full-length LTR (long terminal repeat) retrotransposon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkm286&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d12859e929">25</a></sup>. The SSRs were identified by MIcroSAtellite identification tool (MISA) perl script (<a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/</a>). The minimum number of nucleotide repeats searched during the SSR analysis was set as ten for mono-, six for di-, four for tri- and three for tetra-, penta- and hexa-nucleotide repeats with maximal number of bases interrupting 2 SSRs in a compound microsatellite as 100.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Identification of non-coding RNAs (ncRNA)</h3><p>The ribosomal RNA (rRNA) and small nuclear RNA (snRNA) were identified by INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: Inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btp157&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d12859e948">26</a></sup> with default parameters against Rfam database (release 9.1) (<a href="http://rfam.xfam.org">http://rfam.xfam.org</a>). To predict the miRNAs, we used two steps procedure: first, homology search against Rfam database with a cut-off of 90% identity and query coverage, secondly the presence of hairpin structure in the surrounding sequence of predicted miRNA. The tRNAscan-SE algorithms (Version 1.23)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. TRNAscan-SE: A program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/25.5.0955&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12859e959">27</a></sup> were used to identify and annotate transfer RNA (tRNA) genes with default parameters. For the prediction and annotation of small nucleolar RNA (snoRNA) genes in the assembled genome, snoScan was used with the yeast rRNA methylation sites and yeast rRNA sequences provided by the snoScan distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. A computational screen for methylation guide snoRNAs in Yeast. Science 283, 1168–1171, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1126/science.283.5405.1168&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d12859e963">28</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Phylogenetic analysis with single-copy genes among <i>Oryza</i> species</h3><p>All protein sequences of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome and 11 other sequenced <i>Oryza</i> species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1</a>) were subjected to CD-HIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: A fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658–1659, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btl158&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12859e991">29</a></sup> for clustering at cut-off of 90% coverage and similarity to form unique clusters. Clusters with a single copy gene from all 12 genomes, termed as Single-Copy-gene Clusters, were used for molecular phylogenetic analysis by using the steps as described by Kawano <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kawano, T. et al. Genetic diversity of Entamoeba: Novel ribosomal lineages from cockroaches. PLoS ONE 12, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0185233&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12859e999">30</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Synteny analysis for conserved regions with other species</h3><p>Conserved synteny is used as a measurement for evolutionary divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1153917&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1011">31</a></sup> or as calculation of conserved coding or non-coding region across different genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Haudry, A. et al. An atlas of over 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences provides insight into crucifer regulatory regions. Nat. Genet. 45, 891–898, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1038/ng.2684&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1015">32</a></sup>. To identify the conserved regions across genomes of interest, we performed the synteny analysis of the assembled genome of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> with the reference genome of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> and the model dicot species <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>, along with its wild halophytic relatives <i>Schrenkiella parvula</i> and <i>E</i>. <i>salsugineum</i>. The genome sequences of these 4 species were downloaded from NCBI (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1</a>) and BLAST search was used to find out the conserved regions between <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> assembled genome and these 4 reference genomes with an e-value cut-off of ≤1e<sup>−10</sup>. Homologous blocks of alignment length of ≥500 bp between the references and the assembled genome were rendered in the synteny plot generated using CIRCOS tool v0.67-7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: An information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.092759.109&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1062">33</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Reference-based assembly of chloroplast genome</h3><p>We used the Nanopore long reads to assemble the chloroplast genome of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>. The Nanopore reads were BLAST searched against chloroplast genomes of 11 <i>Oryza</i> species of interest (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1</a>). Out of 1,717,607 Nanopore reads, 28,218 reads (95.26 Mb) were showing significant hits (E-value ≤ 1e<sup>−10</sup>) against <i>Oryza</i> species and were used for 2-round assembly by CLC Genomics Workbench 9.5.1 (CLC Bio, Arhus, Denmark) with chloroplast sequence of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa </i>ssp. <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i> as reference. Circular orientation was checked and chloroplast annotation was done by CpGAVAS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu, C. et al. CpGAVAS, an integrated web server for the annotation, visualization, analysis, and GenBank submission of completely sequenced chloroplast genome sequences. BMC Genomics 13, 715, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2164-13-715&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1105">34</a></sup>. The four junctions between 2 inverted repeat regions and 2 single copy regions were validated by PCR amplification using 4 pairs of primers (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S2</a>). GenBank file was prepared with sequin and subjected to OGDraw v1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lohse, M., Drechsel, O. &amp; Bock, R. OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW): A tool for the easy generation of high-quality custom graphical maps of plastid and mitochondrial genomes. Current Genet. 52, 267–274, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s00294-007-0161-y&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1113">35</a></sup> to generate chloroplast gene map. We also performed the phylogenetic analysis of this assembled chloroplast genome along with chloroplast sequence of 11 other sequenced <i>Oryza</i> species. All 12 sequences were aligned by ClustalW and evolutionary analyses were conducted using the Maximum Likelihood method based on the Tamura-Nei model in MEGA7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis Version 7.0 for Bigger Datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1870–1874, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msw054&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1120">23</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Reference based assembly of mitochondrial genome</h3><p>The processed and quality filtered PE and MP reads were aligned against the mitochondrion genome of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> (NC_011033.1, 490,520 bp) using bowtie2-2.2.5 (<a href="http://bowtie-bio.sourceforge.net/index.shtml">http://bowtie-bio.sourceforge.net/index.shtml</a>) and aligned reads were <i>de novo</i> assembled by Spades-3.9.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nurk, S. et al. Assembling genomes and mini-metagenomes from highly chimeric reads. In Lecture Notes in Computer Science (including subseries Lecture Notes in Artificial Intelligence and Lecture Notes in Bioinformatics). 7821, 158–170, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-642-37195-0_13&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1152">36</a></sup>. The scaffolding and gap closing were done with SSPACE and GapCloser, respectively. The assembled genome was annotated for genes and tRNAs by MITOS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bernt, M. et al. MITOS: Improved de novo metazoan mitochondrial genome annotation. Mol. Phylogenetics Evol. 69, 313–319, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ympev.2012.08.023&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1156">37</a></sup> and MiTFi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Jühling, F. et al. Improved systematic tRNA gene annotation allows new insights into the evolution of mitochondrial tRNA structures and into the mechanisms of mitochondrial genome rearrangements. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 2833–2845, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkr1131&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1160">38</a></sup>, respectively. Phylogenetic analysis was done with 4 other available mitochondrial genomes of <i>Oryza</i> species (<i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica, O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica, O</i>. <i>rufipogon</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>minuta</i>), 2 monocot species (<i>Triticum aestivum</i> and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>) and 2 dicot species (<i>Glycine max</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>) by MEGA7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis Version 7.0 for Bigger Datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1870–1874, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msw054&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1208">23</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Accession Numbers</h3><p>Project Information: NCBI BioProject ID: PRJNA396417. Chloroplast Genome Assembly: GenBank Accession no. MG383937. Mitochondrial genome Assembly: GenBank Accession no. MG429050.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome sequencing, assembly and quality assessment</h3><p>The genome size calculation for <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome from young leaf tissue of pot-grown single plant using <i>P</i>. <i>sativum</i> as an external standard with flow cytometric method resulted in the estimated genome size of 665 Mb (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1a,b</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mondal, T. K., Rawal, H. C., Gaikwad, K., Sharma, T. R. &amp; Singh, N. K. First de novo draft genome sequence of Oryzacoarctata, the only halophytic species in the genus Oryza. F1000 Research 6, 1750, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.12414.1&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1249">39</a></sup>. We have generated a total of 166.69 Gb of HQraw data comprising of short Illumina PEreads (123.78 Gb), MP libraries of 2 Kb (11.12 Gb), 4 Kb (3.06 Gb), 6 Kb (11.23 Gb) and 8 Kb (11.13 Gb) insert size, and Nanopore reads (6.35 Gb) to yield the genome-sequencing depth (x) of 250.66 (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S3</a>). The average read length for Illumina reads were 151 bp, and that for long reads from Nanopore was 3,697 bp with GC% in the range of 42–44% for the sequencing reads generated from each of these technology. The nanopore technology, which was used to generate long reads, was quite successful as more than 60% of the reads having length above 1 Kb, including ~10% above 10 Kb length with maximum read length of 677.71 Kb (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S2a,b</a>). The HQ Illumina PE reads were assembled into 101,992 contigs (555.66 Mb; N50 = 15.13 Kb) using PLATANUS followed by scaffolding with MP and Nanopore reads using SSPACE. The final assembly of 569.99 Mb covering 85.71% of the estimated genome size was obtained after filling the gaps within the scaffolds (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Tab1">1</a>). This final assembly contains 58,362 scaffolds with N50 and L50 values were 1,858,627 bp and 84, respectively. The long reads performed significantly well by reducing 101,992 contigs into 58,362 scaffolds and increased the N50 to ~122 times higher (15.13 Kb to 1.85 Mb). In this final assembly of 569.99 Mb, we achieved the longest contig size of 7.85 Mb with an average scaffold length of 9,766 bp.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of genome assembly, predicted genes and repeats statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Both CEGMA and BUSCO were used to check the completeness of the assembled genome for the presence of core genes. Results from CEGMA, registered for 92.34% (97.18%, partial) completeness of the assembled genome with 229 of 248 ultra-conserved core eukaryotic genes (CEGs) were present in the genome (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S4</a>). The genome completeness raised upto 98.70% when normalized with respect to reference genome of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> spp. <i>japonica</i> Nipponbare-IRGSP-1.0. Similar results were found for the analysis with BUSCO that registered 97.08% completeness with normalized value of 99.43% (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>With high level of completeness for the presence of core genes in the assembled genome, as predicted with CEGMA and BUSCO, we proceed with gene prediction and annotation in the assembled 58,362 scaffolds by integrating AUGUSTUS and MAKER-P annotation pipeline. A 3.32% (1,938) of the total scaffolds were found containing a total of 33,627 predicted protein-coding genes with length ranging from 180 bp to 1926 bp and an average length of 1,147 bp. These numbers are quite comparable with the predicted genes among the 11 other <i>Oryza</i> species sequenced so far (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><p>Of 33,627 predicted genes, a total number of 26,569 (79.01%) were assigned functions based on their significant best BLASTP hits to Plant Genes DB by using Blast2GO. Species distribution showed that majority (i.e. 59.28%) of these 26,569 genes had top BLAST hits against <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i>, followed by <i>O</i>. <i>brachyantha, Aegilops tauschii, Setaria italica</i> and <i>Zea mays</i> with 28.13%, 2.15%, 2.27% and 2.01%, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig1">1a</a>). In total, a good amount of 87.41% genes showed homology against rice and <i>O</i>. <i>brachyantha</i>, in spite of less number of genes from these genomes in the plant gene database used for annotation (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S6</a>), suggesting its existence somewhere between AA and FF genome type or between domesticated and wild relatives. In order to identify the genes associated with biological processes, molecular functions and cellular process, we carried out the functional annotation in terms of Gene Ontology (GO) using BLASTP results. A total of 35,690 GO terms were assigned to 16,357 genes associated with biological processes, cellular components and molecular functions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig1">1b</a>). Among the biological processes, the most prevalent were metabolic process (6,564), cellular process (5,661) and single-organism process (3,169) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig1">1b</a>). Apart from these three generally high-scoring categories, there are 19 more groups with at least 1 or more genes with assigned GO terms but noticeable ones are “response to stimulus” (827 genes), “signalling” (251 genes) and “developmental process” (130 genes). The 827 genes under GO category “response to stimulus” were assigned with 207 GO terms or sub-categories like “response to stress”, “salt-stress”, “oxidative stress”, “water deprivation”, “auxin”, “abscisic acid”, “biotic-” and “abiotic-stimulus” pointing towards the significant presence of stress-related genes in the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome, which are either absent or present in very less number in rice (IRGSP 1.0) (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S7</a>). For cellular processes associated genes, ~40% of genes were assigned GO terms for cell or cell-part category and ~29% genes to that for membrane or membrane-part category (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig1">1b</a>). With 51.59% (8,732 genes), the largest category among genes associated with GO terms for molecular functions was binding activity followed by catalytic activity (35.33%; 5,980 genes) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig1">1b</a>). A total of 2,844 genes were annotated with enzyme code distribution in which high abundance of genes were assigned hydrolases enzyme (1336) and transferases (707) followed by oxidoreductase (404), lyases (155), isomerases (127) and ligases (115). Additionally, 4,640 of the predicted genes were functionally annotated with Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) database. The observed different types in abundance of protein function classes may be important to support different life-styles of the plant species. Finally, there were 7,058 genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> without any significant BLAST hits (E-value ≤ 1e<sup>−10</sup>) against plant genes database and 2,216 of these genes were found neither any significant GO hits nor any InterProScan match which accounts for 22.33% and 6.60% of total genes, respectively indicating that these could act as a gene-pool for <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> specific and unique genes (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="614" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>BLAST based annotation of predicted genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>. (<b>a</b>) Species distribution of 26,569 genes with BLAST hits showed that majority of them (i.e. 59.28%) had top BLAST hits against <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i>ssp. <i>japonica</i>, followed by <i>O</i>. <i>brachyantha, Aegilop stauschii, Setaria italica</i> and <i>Zea mays</i> with28.13%, 2.15%, 2.27% and 2.01%, respectively, (<b>b</b>) GO terms distribution of 26,569 genes in level 2 GO categroization in biological Process, cellular component, and molecular function.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Transcription factors (TF), as key regulator for gene expression, are best suited for understanding the molecular mechanism and incorporating the abiotic stress tolerance in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Nakashima, K., Ito, Y. &amp; Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, K. Transcriptional regulatory networks in response to abiotic stresses in Arabidopsis and grasses. Plant Physiol. 149, 88–95, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.108.129791&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1847">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Urano, K., Kurihara, Y., Seki, M. &amp; Shinozaki, K. “Omics” analyses of regulatory networks in plant abiotic stress responses. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 13, 132–138, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pbi.2009.12.006&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d12859e1850">41</a></sup>. The BLAST based homology search of genes of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> against available plant TFs sequences in PlantTFDB 4.0 identified 4,916 genes (14.62% of total genes) in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> distributed in 56 different TF classes across 133 plant species (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S4</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S9</a>). These 4,916 TFs have a high abundance of the stress-response influencing TFs like MYB or MYB_related (519), NAC (322), bZIP (231), WRKY (177), HB (118), HSF (40), and AP (33). The identified 4,916 TF genes showed homology primarily against <i>O</i>. <i>punctata, O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica, O</i>. <i>meridionalis, O</i>. <i>brachyantha, Malus domestica, O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica, Leersia perrieri, O</i>. <i>glumaepatula, O</i>. <i>nivara, O</i>. <i>longistaminata, Sisymbriumirio, Actinidia chinensis</i> and <i>Fragaria vesca</i> with over 100 genes against each of these plant species (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S9</a>). About 40.30% (1,981) of the identified 8,135 TF genes were showing homology against 11 <i>Oryza</i> species.</p><p>We compared the conserved and unique gene families between <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome and 11 other sequenced <i>Oryza</i> species with representatives from <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica, O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica, O</i>. <i>rufipogon, O</i>. <i>punctata, O</i>. <i>nivara, O</i>. <i>meridionalis, O</i>. <i>longistaminata, O</i>. <i>glumaepatula, O</i>. <i>glaberrima, O</i>. <i>brachyantha</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>barthii</i>. This comparison resulted in a core set of 652,000 genes representing 3,215 clusters shared among all 12 <i>Oryza</i> species, encoding ancestral gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig2">2</a>). We found a total of 20 gene clusters comprising 22 genes unique to the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome followed by <i>O</i>. <i>rufipogon</i> (21 unique gene families: 28 genes), <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> (18 unique gene families: 20 genes), <i>O</i>. <i>glumaepatula</i> (16 unique gene families: 26 genes) and <i>O</i>. <i>glaberrima</i> (11 unique gene families: 29 genes). Interestingly, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp.<i>indica</i> had 468 unique gene families that contain 1,011 genes. The number of unique gene families clusters and gene in <i>O</i>. <i>longistaminata</i> (54 unique gene families: 59 genes)and <i>O</i>. <i>punctate</i> (51 unique gene families: 64 genes) genome were very similar followed by <i>O</i>. <i>meridionalis</i> (43 unique gene families: 59 genes).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="660" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Number of shared and unique gene families among 12 <i>Oryza</i> species including <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome with number of genes within families in parentheses showing a high number of shared genes (652,000) and families (3,215) among these <i>Oryza</i> species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>F-box domain proteins are important for our analysis not only as one of the largest gene family controlling many biological functions but because of its important role in the regulation of various stress-response pathways and developmental processes in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gagne, J. M., Downes, B. P., Shiu, S. H., Durski, A. M. &amp; Vierstra, R. D. The F-box subunit of the SCF E3 complex is encoded by a diverse superfamily of genes in Arabidopsis. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 99, 11519–11524, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.162339999&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2002)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2080">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lechner, E., Achard, P., Vansiri, A., Potuschak, T. &amp; Genschik, P. F-box proteins everywhere. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 9, 631–638, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pbi.2006.09.003&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2006)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2080_1">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xu, G., Ma, H., Nei, M. &amp; Kong, H. Evolution of F-box genes in plants: Different modes of sequence divergence and their relationships with functional diversification. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 835–840, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.0812043106&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2080_2">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Yan, Y. S. et al. Overexpression of an F-box protein gene reduces abiotic stress tolerance and promotes root growth in rice. Mol. Plant 4, 190–197, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/mp/ssq066&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2083">45</a></sup>. We identified 421 F-box genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome using HMMER and BLAST search based approach against F-box domain (PF00646). These F-box genes are about 1.25% of total genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> comparable to those with <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> (2.53%), <i>V</i>. <i>vinifera</i> (0.51%), <i>P</i>. <i>trichocarpa</i> (0.81%), <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> (1.91%), <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> (1.59%), <i>S</i>. <i>moellendorffii</i> (1.11%), and <i>P</i>. <i>patens</i> (0.42%), plant genomes representing different levels of plant kingdoms including a bryophyte, a lycophyte, monocots, and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Schumann, N., Navarro-Quezada, A., Ullrich, K., Kuhl, C. &amp; Quint, M. Molecular evolution and selection patterns of plant F-Box proteins with C-terminal kelch repeats. Plant Physiol. 155(2011), 835–850, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.110.166579&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2144">46</a></sup>.</p><p>There are 3 main genes in the SOS (salt overly sensitive) signal pathway, i.e. <i>SOS1</i>, <i>SOS2</i> and <i>SOS3</i> but as <i>SOS2</i> gets activated by <i>SOS3</i> after interaction, and their complex is required to fully activate <i>SOS1</i>, we focused on the identification of <i>SOS1</i> gene, a set of genes with most important role in plant salt-tolerance, to get an overlay of the abundance of <i>SOS</i> pathway related genes in the assembled genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shi, H., Ishitani, M., Kim, C. &amp; Zhu, J. K. The Arabidopsis thaliana salt tolerance gene SOS1 encodes a putative Na+/H+ antiporter. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 6896–6901, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.120170197&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2000)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2176">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qiu, Q. S., Guo, Y., Dietrich, M. A., Schumaker, K. S. &amp; Zhu, J. K. Regulation of SOS1, a plasma membrane Na+/H+ exchanger in Arabidopsis thaliana, by SOS2 and SOS3. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 99, 8436–8441, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.122224699&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2002)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2176_1">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Halfter, U., Ishitani, M. &amp; Zhu, J. K. The Arabidopsis SOS2 protein kinase physically interacts with and is activated by the calcium-binding protein SOS3. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 3735–3740, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.040577697&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2000)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2176_2">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Yang, Q. et al. Overexpression of SOS (salt overly sensitive) genes increases salt tolerance in transgenic Arabidopsis. Mol. Plant 2, 22–31, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/mp/ssn058&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2179">50</a></sup>. Moreover <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> is halophytic in nature and <i>SOS1</i> was found to have the activity needed for halophytic characteristic as reported in halophytic dicot <i>T</i>. <i>salsuginea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Oh, D. H. et al. Loss of halophytism by interference with SOS1 expression. Plant Physiol. 151, 210–222, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.109.137802&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2198">51</a></sup>. <i>SOS1</i> is one among the very few genes that required for plant salt tolerance which encodes for “plasma membrane Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter”, which not only plays an important role in germination but more importantly aid in the growth of plants in saline conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Mansouri, S., Mehrabi, A. &amp; Kahrizi, D. Phylogenetic analysis of SOS1 gene in different species based on coding sequences. Middle-East J. Sci. 14, 1226–1229, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.5829/idosi.mejsr.2013.14.9.2154&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2210">52</a></sup>. So we focused on “Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter” domain or keyword based strategy to identify <i>SOS1</i> genes, as described in methodology. We identified a total of 8 <i>SOS1</i> genes in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome either by homology search against known <i>SOS1</i> genes or BLAST based function annotation of predicted genes and with the presence of “Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter or exchanger family” domain. To classify these eight <i>SOS1</i> genes in to plasma membrane Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> or vacuolar Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter gene, we have chosen 18 <i>SOS1</i> genes as used by Chen <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Chen, X. et al. GhSOS1, a plasma membrane Na+/H+ antiporter gene from upland cotton, enhances salt tolerance in transgenic Arabidopsis thaliana. PloS One 12, e0181450, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0181450&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2257">53</a></sup> and are available at NCBI with Accession numbers as: AF256224, AF510074, AB439132, GU188850, KC410809, KJ577576, EU780458, AY785147, AB021878, DQ517530, FJ866610, DQ071264, EU879059, AY131235, AB198179, KF914414, EF207775, EU333827, representing 11 plasma membrane and 7 vacuolar Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter genes from different plant species including <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana, Chrysanthemum crassum, Halostachys caspica, Helianthus tuberosus, Kosteletzkya virginica, Limonium gmelinii, O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica, Populus euphratica, Pennisetum glaucum, Salicornia brachiata, Salicornia europaea, Suaeda japonica, S. salsa, T</i>. <i>halophile</i> and <i>Zoysia japonica</i>. A multiple sequence alignment and phylogenetic analysis was performed between these sequences and the close analysis of so obtained Neighbour Joining (NJ) tree suggested that only 1 of the 8 <i>SOS1</i> genes (<i>OcSOS1-1</i>) clustered with plasma-membrane Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporters of other plant species while rest of the 7<i>SOS1</i> genes (<i>OcSOS1-2,3,4,5,6,7,8</i>) were part of the cluster having vacuolar Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporters from other plant species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig3">3</a>). But in both clusters, these genes were closely related with <i>SOS1</i> homologs of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i>. Three of these identified <i>SOS1</i> genes i.e. <i>OcSOS1-6, OcSOS1-7</i> and <i>OcSOS1-8</i> could be the newly evolving ones as they showed a very low or no homology against known SOS1 genes and were present in a separate cluster in the obtained tree.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="671" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The phylogenetic analysis of <i>OcSOS1</i> genes with some other representative plant species to classify the identified 8 <i>SOS1</i> genes in to plasma membrane or vacuolar Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Analysis of repetitive elements, SSRs and ncRNAs</h3><p>A total of 113,352,647 bp (19.89%) of the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome was estimated to consist of repeated sequences (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Tab1">1</a>). We were able to identify 129,734 (14.95%) retrotransposons (class I), 79,468 (3.53%) DNA transposons (class II) and 3,092 (0.16%) unclassified elements in the genome (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S10</a>).Among retroelements, 14.48% (82.53 Mb) of genome accounts for LTR retrotransposons and just 0.47% for Non-LTRs (SINEs and LINEs). The copia and gypsy are the two main components of LTR retrotransposons accounting 8.24% (46.94 Mb) and 6.04% (34.42 Mb) of the assembled genome, respectively. With LTR_FINDER, we have identified 218 full length LTR retrotransposons in 142 scaffolds (out of total 58362 scaffolds) comprising of a total of 1.30 Mb which accounts just 0.23% of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S10</a>).</p><p>A total of 230,968 SSRs were obtained across genome with an average frequency of one SSR per 2.46 kb. Out of total 58,362 scaffolds, 12,926 (22.15%) were found to contain SSR and 5,171 of these had more than one SSR (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S11</a>). Among these identified SSRs, tetranucleotides (27.64%) were highest followed by trinucleotides (26.66%), mono- (19.99%), di- (16.79%), penta- (5.92%) and hexa-nucleotides (3.00%) (Fig <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S5a</a>). For mono-, di- and tri- types of SSRs A/T, AT/TA, and CCG/CGG, were the motifs with highest percentages of 16.86%, 10.28% and 7.54%, respectively (Fig <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S5b</a>).</p><p>Non-coding RNA genes have important regulatory roles in a number of plant phenomenons like chromosomal silencing, regulating the transcription process, developmental control, and various stress-responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="MacIntosh, G. C., Wilkerson, C. &amp; Green, P. J. Identification and analysis of Arabidopsis expressed sequence tags characteristic of non-coding RNAs. Plant Physiol. 127, 765–776, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.010501.cient&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2001)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2404">54</a></sup>. Different types of ncRNAs were identified in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome included miRNA, rRNA, snRNA, tRNA and snoRNA (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S12</a>).
