The <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome was found to have 200 miRNA genes, 118 snRNA genes and 3,110 rRNA (LSU, SSU, 5_8S_rRNA and 5S_rRNA) genes encoded by “cmsearch” module of the INFERNAL package using the relevant covariance model from Rfam. The tRNAScan-SE algorithms, as applied with default parameters to the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome assembly, resulted in the identification of 900 putative tRNAs in the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> assembly. The snoRNA is a small RNA molecule that leads the chemical modifications of other RNAs, including rRNAs, tRNAs and snRNAs. In total 1,184 snoRNA were identified in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Single copy genes: Phylogenetic analysis of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> along with other 11 <i>Oryza</i> species</h3><p>In order to perform the genome level phylogenetic analysis of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> along with other 11 <i>Oryza</i> species, a total of 194,069 unique clusters were formed with a cut-off of 90% for coverage and similarity from their protein sequences. The cluster analysis resulted in total of 170 putative single copy gene clusters. These single copy proteins from the 12 genomes were considered for phylogenetic analysis. The tree generated with these single-copy orthologous genes placed <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> as an individual clade and was found consistent with the species-tree obtained from the TimeTree database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Hedges, S. B., Marin, J., Suleski, M., Paymer, M. &amp; Kumar, S. Tree of life reveals clock-like speciation and diversification. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 835–845, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msv037&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2477">55</a></sup>, which generally used to retrieved the divergence times among species. A consensus analysis based on both the tree depict that FF genome (<i>O</i>. <i>brachyantha</i>) diverged about 15 million years ago (MYA) followed by <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> around 10 MYA and the BB genome (<i>O</i>. <i>punctata</i>) about 6 MYA from the AA genome types. Among AA type genomes, it seems that Australian species, <i>O</i>. <i>meridionlais</i> was first to diverge from other AA genome species (South-American, Asian and African) and that happened somewhere around 2–3 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="773" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Tree topology of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> along with 11 other Oryza species based on the 170 clusters derived from 1:1 single-copy orthologous genes to depict the phylogeny at genome level to have an estimate of evolution of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> along with AA, BB and FF genome types.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Synteny analysis</h3><p>For representation of homologous blocks in synteny plots, unplaced scaffolds from each reference were concatenated and represented as single contig (“Un”) and the contigs with less than 1MB size in our assembled genome were concatenated and are represented as a single scaffold (“Unplaced”) (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S6</a>). In the synteny analysis of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> with three of the dicot species including <i>A</i>.<i>thaliana</i>, <i>E</i>. <i>salsugineum</i> and <i>S</i>. <i>parvula</i>,high numbers of syntenic blocks were found with just 1 scaffold (S128; 1,335,219 bp) of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> having no collinear or syntenic block with any of these three species (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S6a–c</a>). On the other hand, in spite of large genome size and more number of chromosomes in <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i>, there were 8 scaffolds of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> showing no collinearity or synteny with <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S6d</a>) indicating that these scaffolds, which account for 12.52 Mb i.e. 2.19% of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome, might have some possible roles in halophytic adaptation of this species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Analysis of chloroplast genome</h3><p>The chloroplast genome exists as circular molecule in angiosperms with size ranging from 120 to 160 kb in length<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Clegg, M. T., Gaut, B. S., Learn, G. H. &amp; Morton, B. R. Rates and patterns of chloroplast DNA evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 91, 6795–67801, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.91.15.6795&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1994)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2628">56</a></sup>. We used 11 chloroplast sequences of <i>Oryza</i> species with size ranging from 134,558 bp to 135,525 bp as reference (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S1</a>). Chloroplast reads were extracted from Nanopore data by BLAST search and assembled by guidance-based assembly into a circular contig of 134,750 bp length. It has a typical and standard quadripartite plant chloroplast structure with comparable regions of ~20.8 kb, ~80.8 kb and ~12.3 kb inverted repeats (IR), large single copy (LSC) and small single copy (SSC) regions, respectively with that of other <i>Oryza</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Clegg, M. T., Gaut, B. S., Learn, G. H. &amp; Morton, B. R. Rates and patterns of chloroplast DNA evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 91, 6795–67801, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.91.15.6795&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1994)." href="#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2641">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wicke, S., Schneeweiss, G. M., dePamphilis, C. W., Müller, K. F. &amp; Quandt, D. The evolution of the plastid chromosome in land plants: Gene content, gene order, gene function. Plant Mol. Biol. 76, 273–297, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s11103-011-9762-4&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2011)." href="#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2641_1">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Asaf, S. et al. The complete chloroplast genome of wild rice (Oryzaminuta) and its comparison to related species. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 304, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2017.00304&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2644">58</a></sup>. These four junctions were further confirmed and validated by PCR amplification (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S2</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S7</a>). While the IRa and IRb spanned for 20,800 bp region of assembled chloroplast, the LSC and SSC regions covered 80,816 bp and 12,334 bp, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S13</a>). There were 82 protein coding genes, 33 tRNA genes and 8 rRNA genes, making a total of 123 genes with 18 genes as one copy in each of the 2 IR regions (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S13</a>). In total, it has 43 genes involved in photosynthesis and divided in 6 different classes (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S14</a>). No <i>ycf1</i> gene was found in this newly assembled chloroplast which supports the early loss of this gene in <i>Poaceae</i> family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Asaf, S. et al. The complete chloroplast genome of wild rice (Oryzaminuta) and its comparison to related species. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 304, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2017.00304&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2674">58</a></sup>. The phylogenetic analysis with chloroplast genomes of 11 other <i>Oryza</i> species infer that this newly assembled chloroplast genome is close to <i>japonica</i> subgroup of <i>Oryza</i> and is a part of the clade consisting of AA genome type while the BB and FF genome type, i.e. <i>punctata</i> and <i>brachyantha</i>, respectively were representing single different clades each (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig5">5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1045" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Genome organization and phylogeny of chloroplast genome of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>. (<b>a</b>) the chloroplast genome organization showing the genes transcribed clockwise (drawn inside the circle) and counter clockwise (drawn outside) with different gene functional groups are colour coded. (<b>b</b>) phylogenetic relationship of 12 <i>Oryza</i> species based upon whole choloroplast genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Analysis of mitochondrial genome</h3><p>The assembled mitochondrial genome is 491,065 bp long (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig6">6a</a>) with 43.07% of GC content comprising of 46 protein coding genes, 37 tRNA genes and 4 rRNA genes (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S15</a>). The annotation also confirm the presence of 4 genes related to functional category ‘Origins of replication’, making a total of 91 genes in the mitochondrial genome with a total of 48,960 bp (i.e.9.97% of assembled mitochondrial genome). The genome organization is comparable with available mitochondrial genomes of 4 other <i>Oryza</i> species with almost similar GC content and same range of protein coding genes, tRNA genes and rRNAgenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Nakai, M. YCF1: A Green TIC: Response to the de Vries et al. Commentary: Fig. 1. The Plant Cell 27, 1834–1838, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.15.00363&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2747">59</a></sup>. The annotated 46 protein coding genes here, included 25 genes related to the production of ATP synthase and the electron transport chain with 9, 1, 3 and 5 subunits of complex I (<i>nad1-9</i>), III (<i>cob</i>), IV (<i>cox1-3</i>) and V (<i>atp1,4,6,8,9</i>), respectively (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S16</a>). Besides, there were 11 ribosomal proteins and only a single maturase (<i>mat-r</i>) gene.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-31518-y/MediaObjects/41598_2018_31518_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1058" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Assembly and phylogeny of mitochondrial genome of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>. (<b>a</b>) Circular representation of the <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> mitochondrial genome showing GC content and identity with reference mitochondrial genome of <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> at each base level. (<b>b</b>) Phylogenetic relationship of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> along with other species from Poaceae family and some other monocot and dicot species at mitochondrial genome level.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To understand the evolutionary relationship of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i>, at mitochondrial level, with other <i>Oryza</i> species, the mitochondrial genome sequences of 4 members of <i>Poaceae</i> family (<i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica, O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica, O</i>. <i>rufipogon</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>minuta</i>), 2 other monocot species (<i>T</i>. <i>aestivum</i> and <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i>) and 2 dicot species (<i>G</i>. <i>max</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>) were aligned to the newly assembled mitochondria genome of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> to construct a NJ tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#Fig6">6b</a>). On one hand, this phylogenetic analysis with NJ tree clearly separates <i>Poaceae</i> family members from other 4 species included in the analysis and on the other hand, it placed <i>O</i>. <i>rufipogon</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>minuta</i> in one clade while <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>indica</i> in other clade as reported earlier by researchers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Asaf, S. et al. Mitochondrial genome analysis of wild rice (Oryzaminuta) and its comparison with other related species. PLoS ONE 11, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0152937&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2932">60</a></sup>. But most importantly, we found <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> in a group of <i>Poaceae</i> family, but in a different clade and closely related to the reference genome i.e. <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec24" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec24-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec24">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec24-content"><p>We report first time the draft genome sequence of a monocot halophyte, <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> which provides an excellent platform for exploring the genetic potential of this wild species of rice. Our flow cytometry based results indicated the genome size be of 665 Mb, which is much closer to the previously reported estimated size of 771 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kim, H. et al. Construction, alignment and analysis of twelve framework physical maps that represent the ten genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Biol. 9, R45, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/gb-2008-9-2-r45&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d12859e2970">61</a></sup>. The results demonstrate several other interesting biological aspects and features of the genome. The Nanopore long reads significantly improved almost all the metrics related to quality of assembly, including reduced number of scaffolds, large size of the scaffolds (upto 7.85 Mb), a significant N50 (1.85 Mb) or L50 value, a good range of mean and median lengths (9.76 kb) along with a high percentage of genome completeness measure (upto 97.08%) based on the presence of core orthologous genes and hence contributed in the best representation of genome. It also helped us to retrieve the complete set of chloroplast genome sequence. To date, this is the only largest sequenced genome of any halophyte with the deepest sequencing coverage of 250.66-fold, considering the available sequenced halophytic genome such as <i>T</i>. <i>parvula</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>salsuginea</i> and/or <i>E</i>. <i>salsugineum</i> (Supplemental Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#MOESM1">S17</a>). A total of 33,627 predicted protein-coding genes that we discovered here, are the highest among sequenced halophytes and are comparable with other <i>Oryza</i> species. A 22.33% of these genes were found specific and unique to <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> but rest were showing a good homology with rice (domesticated and AA type) and <i>O</i>. <i>brachyantha</i> (wild and FF type). The small genomes of chloroplast and mitochondria aid well in understanding the evolutionary background of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> in <i>Oryza</i> genus and among with AA, BB and FF genome type and supports the conservative nature of chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes within the taxon. Further, a phylogenetic analysis based on the single copy genes among <i>Oryza</i> species pointing towards the existence of <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> genome somewhere between the divergence of FF and BB genome form AA genome. It could be true as assumed by researchers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Sanchez, P. L., Wing, R. A. &amp; Brar, D. S. The wild relative of Rice: Genomes and genomics. In: Genetics and Genomics of Rice 9–25, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4614-7903-1_2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3031">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. D. et al. The Oryza BAC resource: A genus-wide and genome scale tool for exploring rice genome evolution and leveraging useful genetic diversity from wild relatives. Breeding Sci. 60, 536–543, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1270/jsbbs.60.536&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3034">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ge, S., Sang, T., Lu, B. R. &amp; Hong, D. Y. Phylogeny of rice genomes with emphasis on origins of allotetraploid species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 96, 14400–14405, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.96.25.14400&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3037">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. Spatio-temporal patterns of genome evolution in allotetraploid species of the genus Oryza. Plant J. 63, 430–442, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2010.04251.x&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3040">63</a></sup> but needs to be validated with further study.</p><p>Further, when compared with rice, there were found 123 GO terms including “response to salt-stress”, “abscisic acid”, “biotic stress”, “desiccation”, “defence response to fungus”, etc., under GO category “response to stimulus”, exclusively present in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> but completely absent or present in low number in rice favouring the high salinity adaptation of this species. Moreover the presence of 8 <i>SOS1</i> gene copies in <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> mayfavours and ads up to its salinity tolerance mechanism as <i>SOS1</i>contribute to salt tolerance by pumping the sodium ions out of the cells once activated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Yang, Q. et al. Overexpression of SOS (salt overly sensitive) genes increases salt tolerance in transgenic Arabidopsis. Mol. Plant 2, 22–31, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/mp/ssn058&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3066">50</a></sup>. Even the total identified (4,916) and stress-responsive TFs (1,440) were much larger in number than those found in rice (2,478 and 1,408, respectively) in earlier reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Priya, P. &amp; Jain, M. RiceSRTFDB: A database of rice transcription factors containing comprehensive expression, cis-regulatory element and mutant information to facilitate gene function analysis. Database 2013, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/database/bat027&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3070">64</a></sup>. <i>Oryza coarctata</i>, even being a tetraploid, is found to have low repetitive content which is an exception and similar results were found reported by earlier researchers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Gill, N. et al. Dynamic Oryza genomes: repetitive DNA sequences as genome modeling agents. Rice 3, 251–269, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s12284-010-9054-7&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3077">65</a></sup>. Although 82.53 Mb (14.48%) of genome consists of LTR retrotransposons but only 1.30 Mb (i.e. 0.23% of genome) of them were found as full-length LTRs, suggesting the probably high occurrences of recombination or deletion events post retrotransposition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Jiang, S. Y. &amp; Ramachandran, S. Genome-Wide Survey and Comparative Analysis of LTR Retrotransposons and Their Captured Genes in Rice and Sorghum. PLoS ONE 8, e71118, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0071118&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-31518-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d12859e3081">66</a></sup>. Comparatively a little higher synteny was found for <i>O</i>. <i>coarctata</i> with <i>E</i>. <i>salsugineum</i> and <i>S</i>. <i>parvula</i> as compared to rice, which could be due to the halophytic nature of these species indicating that, this genome sequence offers advantages as an ideal system for functional genomics to understand the salinity tolerance mechanisms of monocots as rest halophyte sequenced genome are dicot species. More importantly, our genome information will complement the I-OMAP project along with already existing genomic resources of different wild and cultivated <i>Oryza</i> species and will aid in both the genome level and the salinity based comparative genomics, evolutionary studies and extension of gene pool for improvement in cultivated rice.</p></div></div></section>
